	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Library of Congress</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the 1945 documentary film, see <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress_(film)" title="Library of Congress (film)">Library of Congress (film)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">(de facto) national library of the United States of America</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LOC_Main_Reading_Room_Highsmith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/LOC_Main_Reading_Room_Highsmith.jpg/300px-LOC_Main_Reading_Room_Highsmith.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/LOC_Main_Reading_Room_Highsmith.jpg/450px-LOC_Main_Reading_Room_Highsmith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/LOC_Main_Reading_Room_Highsmith.jpg/600px-LOC_Main_Reading_Room_Highsmith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6144" data-file-height="4098" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LOC_Main_Reading_Room_Highsmith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main reading room at the Library of Congress</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Library of Congress</b> (<b>LOC</b>) is the research library that officially serves the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> and is the <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> national library of the United States. It is the oldest federal cultural institution in the United States. The library is housed in three buildings on <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Hill" title="Capitol Hill">Capitol Hill</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>; it also maintains the <a href="/wiki/National_Audio-Visual_Conservation_Center" title="National Audio-Visual Conservation Center">National Audio-Visual Conservation Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Culpeper,_Virginia" title="Culpeper, Virginia">Culpeper, Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fascinating_Facts_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fascinating_Facts-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The library's functions are overseen by the <a href="/wiki/Librarian_of_Congress" title="Librarian of Congress">librarian of Congress</a>, and its buildings are maintained by the <a href="/wiki/Architect_of_the_Capitol" title="Architect of the Capitol">architect of the Capitol</a>. The Library of Congress claims to be the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_libraries" title="List of largest libraries">largest library in the world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_Britannica:_Library_of_Congress_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_Britannica:_Library_of_Congress-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FFStats_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FFStats-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Its "collections are universal, not limited by subject, format, or national boundary, and include research materials from all parts of the world and in more than 450 languages."<sup id="cite_ref-Fascinating_Facts_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fascinating_Facts-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Congress moved to Washington, D.C., in 1800 after sitting for 11 years in the temporary national capitals in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>. The small Congressional Library was housed in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a> for most of the 19th century until the early 1890s. Most of the original collection had been destroyed by the British in 1814 during the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, and the library sought to restore its collection in 1815. They bought <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>'s entire personal collection of 6,487 books.  After a period of slow growth, another fire struck the library in its Capitol chambers in 1851, again destroying a large amount of the collection, including many of Jefferson's books.  After the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, the Library of Congress grew rapidly in both size and importance, which sparked a campaign to purchase replacement copies for volumes that had been burned. The library received the right of transference of all copyrighted works to deposit two copies of books, maps, illustrations, and diagrams printed in the United States. It also began to build its collections, and its development culminated between 1888 and 1894 with the construction of a separate, extensive library building across the street from the Capitol.
</p><p>The library's primary mission is to research inquiries made by members of Congress, carried out through the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Research_Service" title="Congressional Research Service">Congressional Research Service</a>. The library is open to the public, although only high-ranking government officials and library employees may check out books and materials.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Loc_contruction.jpg" class="image"><img alt="indigo progress construction photographs of the Library of Congress Jefferson Building" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Loc_contruction.jpg/220px-Loc_contruction.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="569" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Loc_contruction.jpg/330px-Loc_contruction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7b/Loc_contruction.jpg 2x" data-file-width="406" data-file-height="1050" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Loc_contruction.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Construction of the <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Building" title="Thomas Jefferson Building">Thomas Jefferson Building</a> from July 8, 1888 to May 15, 1894</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="1800.E2.80.931851:_Origin_and_Jefferson.27s_contribution"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1800–1851:_Origin_and_Jefferson's_contribution">1800–1851: Origin and Jefferson's contribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1800–1851: Origin and Jefferson&#039;s contribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> is credited with the idea of creating a congressional library, first making such a proposition in 1783.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The Library of Congress was subsequently established April 24, 1800 when President <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> signed an act of Congress providing for the transfer of the seat of government from <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> to the new capital city of Washington. Part of the legislation appropriated $5,000 "for the purchase of such books as may be necessary for the use of Congress ... and for fitting up a suitable apartment for containing them."<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Books were ordered from London, and the collection consisted of 740 books and three maps which were housed in the new <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-loc_history_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc_history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>President <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> played an important role in establishing the structure of the Library of Congress. On January 26, 1802, he signed a bill that allowed the president to appoint the <a href="/wiki/Librarian_of_Congress" title="Librarian of Congress">librarian of Congress</a> and establishing a <a href="/wiki/Joint_Committee_on_the_Library" class="mw-redirect" title="Joint Committee on the Library">Joint Committee on the Library</a> to regulate and oversee it. The new law also extended borrowing privileges to the president and vice president.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The invading British army <a href="/wiki/Burning_of_Washington" title="Burning of Washington">burned Washington</a> in August 1814 during the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> and destroyed the Library of Congress and its collection of 3,000 volumes.<sup id="cite_ref-loc_history_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc_history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> These volumes had been left in the Senate wing of the Capitol.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> One of the few congressional volumes to survive was a government account book of receipts and expenditures for 1810.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It was taken as a souvenir by British admiral <a href="/wiki/Sir_George_Cockburn,_10th_Baronet" title="Sir George Cockburn, 10th Baronet">George Cockburn</a>, whose family returned it to the United States government in 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Within a month, Thomas Jefferson offered to sell his personal library<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> as a replacement. Congress accepted his offer in January 1815, appropriating $23,950 to purchase his 6,487 books.<sup id="cite_ref-loc_history_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc_history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Some members of the House of Representatives opposed the outright purchase, including New Hampshire representative <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Webster" title="Daniel Webster">Daniel Webster</a> who wanted to return "all books of an atheistical, irreligious, and immoral tendency."<sup id="cite_ref-:3_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Jefferson had spent 50 years accumulating a wide variety of books in several languages and on subjects such as philosophy, history, law, religion, architecture, travel, natural sciences, mathematics, studies of classical Greece and Rome, modern inventions, hot air balloons, music, submarines, fossils, agriculture, and meteorology.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> He had also collected books on topics not normally viewed as part of a legislative library, such as cookbooks. However, he believed that all subjects had a place in the Library of Congress. He remarked:
</p>
<blockquote><p>I do not know that it contains any branch of science which Congress would wish to exclude from their collection; there is, in fact, no subject to which a Member of Congress may not have occasion to refer.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Jefferson's collection was unique in that it was the working collection of a scholar, not a gentleman's collection for display. With the addition of his collection, the Library of Congress was transformed from a specialist's library to a more general one.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> His original collection was organized into a scheme based on <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Works_by_Francis_Bacon#Scientific_works" title="Works by Francis Bacon">organization of knowledge</a>. Specifically, he grouped his books into Memory, Reason, and Imagination, which broke down into 44 more subdivisions. The library followed Jefferson's organization scheme until the late 19th century, when librarian <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Putnam" title="Herbert Putnam">Herbert Putnam</a> began work on a more flexible Library of Congress Classification structure that now applies to more than 138 million items.
</p><p>In 1851, a fire destroyed two thirds of the Jefferson collection, with only 2,000 books remaining. By 2008, the librarians of Congress had found replacements for all but 300 of the works that were in Jefferson's original collection.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1851.E2.80.931865:_Weakening"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1851–1865:_Weakening">1851–1865: Weakening</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1851–1865: Weakening">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On December 22, 1851, the largest fire in the library's history destroyed 35,000 books, about two–thirds of the library's collection and two-thirds of Jefferson's original transfer.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Congress appropriated $168,700 to replace the lost books in 1852 but not to acquire new materials.<sup id="cite_ref-Library_of_Congress_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Library_of_Congress-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>  This marked the start of a conservative period in the library's administration by librarian <a href="/wiki/John_Silva_Meehan" title="John Silva Meehan">John Silva Meehan</a> and joint committee chairman <a href="/wiki/James_A._Pearce" class="mw-redirect" title="James A. Pearce">James A. Pearce</a>, who restricted the library's activities.<sup id="cite_ref-Library_of_Congress_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Library_of_Congress-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Meehan and Pearce's views about a restricted scope for the Library of Congress reflected those shared by members of Congress. While Meehan was librarian he supported and perpetuated the notion that "the congressional library should play a limited role on the national scene and that its collections, by and large, should emphasize American materials of obvious use to the U.S. Congress."<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In 1859, Congress transferred the library's public document distribution activities to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Interior" title="United States Department of the Interior">Department of the Interior</a> and its international book exchange program to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">Department of State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1850s, <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> librarian <a href="/wiki/Charles_Coffin_Jewett" title="Charles Coffin Jewett">Charles Coffin Jewett</a> aggressively tried to make the Smithsonian into the United States' national library. His efforts were blocked by Smithsonian secretary <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Henry" title="Joseph Henry">Joseph Henry</a>, who advocated a focus on scientific research and publication.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> To reinforce his intentions for the Smithsonian, Henry established laboratories, developed a robust physical sciences library and started the <i><a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Contributions_to_Knowledge" class="mw-redirect" title="Smithsonian Contributions to Knowledge">Smithsonian Contributions to Knowledge</a></i>, the first of many publications intended to disseminate research results.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> For Henry, the Library of Congress was the obvious choice as the national library. Unable to resolve the conflict, Henry dismissed Jewett in July 1854. In 1865 the Smithsonian building, also called the Castle due to its Norman architectural style, was devastated by fire and presented Henry an opportunity in regards to the Smithsonian's non-scientific library. Around this time, the Library of Congress was making plans to build and relocate to the new <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Building" title="Thomas Jefferson Building">Thomas Jefferson Building</a>, which would be fire proof.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Authorized by an act of Congress, he transferred the Smithsonian's non-scientific library of 40,000 volumes to the Library of Congress in 1866.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> appointed <a href="/wiki/John_G._Stephenson" class="mw-redirect" title="John G. Stephenson">John G. Stephenson</a> as librarian of Congress in 1861 and the appointment is regarded as the most political to date.<sup id="cite_ref-John_G_Stephenson_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_G_Stephenson-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Stephenson was a physician and spent equal time serving as librarian and as a physician in the Union Army. He could manage this division of interest because he hired <a href="/wiki/Ainsworth_Rand_Spofford" title="Ainsworth Rand Spofford">Ainsworth Rand Spofford</a> as his assistant.<sup id="cite_ref-John_G_Stephenson_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_G_Stephenson-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Despite his new job, Stephenson's focus was on non-library affairs; three weeks into his term, he left Washington, D.C. to serve as a volunteer aide-de-camp at the battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chancellorsville" title="Battle of Chancellorsville">Chancellorsville</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gettysburg" title="Battle of Gettysburg">Gettysburg</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-John_G_Stephenson_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_G_Stephenson-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Stephenson's term as librarian seems to have left little imprint on the library although hiring Spofford, who was left to run the library in his absence, may have been his most significant achievement.<sup id="cite_ref-John_G_Stephenson_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_G_Stephenson-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Library_of_Congress_in_Capitol_Building.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Library of Congress stacks in the Capitol building" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Library_of_Congress_in_Capitol_Building.jpg/220px-Library_of_Congress_in_Capitol_Building.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Library_of_Congress_in_Capitol_Building.jpg/330px-Library_of_Congress_in_Capitol_Building.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/Library_of_Congress_in_Capitol_Building.jpg 2x" data-file-width="335" data-file-height="437" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Library_of_Congress_in_Capitol_Building.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Library of Congress inside the Capitol Building c. 1890</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="1865.E2.80.931897:_Spofford.27s_expansion"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1865–1897:_Spofford's_expansion">1865–1897: Spofford's expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1865–1897: Spofford&#039;s expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Library of Congress reasserted itself during the latter half of the 19th century under Librarian <a href="/wiki/Ainsworth_Rand_Spofford" title="Ainsworth Rand Spofford">Ainsworth Rand Spofford</a> who directed it from 1865 to 1897. He built broad bipartisan support for it as a national library and a legislative resource, aided by an overall expansion of the federal government and a favorable political climate. He began comprehensively collecting <a href="/wiki/Americana" title="Americana">Americana</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_literature" title="American literature">American literature</a>, led the construction of a new building to house the library, and transformed the librarian of Congress position into one of strength and independence.  Between 1865 and 1870, Congress appropriated funds for the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Building" title="Thomas Jefferson Building">Thomas Jefferson Building</a>, placed all copyright registration and deposit activities under the library's control, and restored the international book exchange. The library also acquired the vast libraries of the Smithsonian and of historian <a href="/wiki/Peter_Force" title="Peter Force">Peter Force</a>, strengthening its scientific and Americana collections significantly. By 1876, the Library of Congress had 300,000 volumes and was tied with the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Public_Library" title="Boston Public Library">Boston Public Library</a> as the nation's largest library. It moved from the Capitol building to its new headquarters in 1897 with more than 840,000 volumes, 40 percent of which had been acquired through copyright deposit.<sup id="cite_ref-loc_history_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc_history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hl026001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="piles of books waiting to be shelved in Thomas Jefferson building" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Hl026001.jpg/220px-Hl026001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Hl026001.jpg/330px-Hl026001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Hl026001.jpg/440px-Hl026001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="572" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hl026001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some of the Library of Congress' holdings awaiting shelving inside the newly opened Thomas Jefferson Building in 1897</div></div></div>
<p>A year before the library's move to its new location, the Joint Library Committee held a session of hearings to assess the condition of the library and plan for its future growth and possible reorganization. Spofford and six experts sent by the <a href="/wiki/American_Library_Association" title="American Library Association">American Library Association</a><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> testified that the library should continue its expansion towards becoming a true national library. Congress more than doubled the library's staff from 42 to 108 based on the hearings, and with the assistance of senators <a href="/wiki/Justin_Morrill" class="mw-redirect" title="Justin Morrill">Justin Morrill</a> of Vermont and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_W._Voorhees" title="Daniel W. Voorhees">Daniel W. Voorhees</a> of Indiana, and established new administrative units for all aspects of the collection. Congress also strengthened the office of Librarian of Congress to govern the library and make staff appointments, as well as requiring Senate approval for presidential appointees to the position.<sup id="cite_ref-loc_history_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc_history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1897.E2.80.931939:_Post-reorganization"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1897–1939:_Post-reorganization">1897–1939: Post-reorganization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1897–1939: Post-reorganization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Library_of_Congress,_Washington-LCCN2008678216_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="bird&#39;s eye view color postcard of Library of Congress Jefferson building" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/The_Library_of_Congress%2C_Washington-LCCN2008678216_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-The_Library_of_Congress%2C_Washington-LCCN2008678216_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/The_Library_of_Congress%2C_Washington-LCCN2008678216_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-The_Library_of_Congress%2C_Washington-LCCN2008678216_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/The_Library_of_Congress%2C_Washington-LCCN2008678216_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-The_Library_of_Congress%2C_Washington-LCCN2008678216_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="994" data-file-height="729" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Library_of_Congress,_Washington-LCCN2008678216_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Library of Congress in 1902</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Reading_Room,_with_murals_by_Ezra_Winter._Library_of_Congress_John_Adams_Building,_Washington,_D.C._LCCN2007687090.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/South_Reading_Room%2C_with_murals_by_Ezra_Winter._Library_of_Congress_John_Adams_Building%2C_Washington%2C_D.C._LCCN2007687090.tif/lossy-page1-220px-South_Reading_Room%2C_with_murals_by_Ezra_Winter._Library_of_Congress_John_Adams_Building%2C_Washington%2C_D.C._LCCN2007687090.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/South_Reading_Room%2C_with_murals_by_Ezra_Winter._Library_of_Congress_John_Adams_Building%2C_Washington%2C_D.C._LCCN2007687090.tif/lossy-page1-330px-South_Reading_Room%2C_with_murals_by_Ezra_Winter._Library_of_Congress_John_Adams_Building%2C_Washington%2C_D.C._LCCN2007687090.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/South_Reading_Room%2C_with_murals_by_Ezra_Winter._Library_of_Congress_John_Adams_Building%2C_Washington%2C_D.C._LCCN2007687090.tif/lossy-page1-440px-South_Reading_Room%2C_with_murals_by_Ezra_Winter._Library_of_Congress_John_Adams_Building%2C_Washington%2C_D.C._LCCN2007687090.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7240" data-file-height="4982" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Reading_Room,_with_murals_by_Ezra_Winter._Library_of_Congress_John_Adams_Building,_Washington,_D.C._LCCN2007687090.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adams Building (opened 1939) – South Reading Room, with murals by <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Winter" title="Ezra Winter">Ezra Winter</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Library of Congress, spurred by the 1897 reorganization, began to grow and develop more rapidly. Spofford's successor <a href="/wiki/John_Russell_Young" title="John Russell Young">John Russell Young</a>, though only in office for two years, overhauled the library's bureaucracy, used his connections as a former diplomat to acquire more materials from around the world, and established the library's first assistance programs for the <a href="/wiki/Blindness" class="mw-redirect" title="Blindness">blind</a> and physically disabled.  Young's successor <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Putnam" title="Herbert Putnam">Herbert Putnam</a> held the office for forty years from 1899 to 1939, entering into the position two years before the library became the first in the United States to hold one million volumes.<sup id="cite_ref-loc_history_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc_history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Putnam focused his efforts on making the library more accessible and useful for the public and for other libraries. He instituted the interlibrary loan service, transforming the Library of Congress into what he referred to as a "library of last resort".<sup id="cite_ref-interlibrary_loan_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-interlibrary_loan-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Putnam also expanded library access to "scientific investigators and duly qualified individuals" and began publishing <a href="/wiki/Primary_sources" class="mw-redirect" title="Primary sources">primary sources</a> for the benefit of scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-loc_history_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc_history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Putnam's tenure also saw increasing diversity in the library's acquisitions. In 1903, he persuaded President <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> to transfer by executive order the papers of the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers</a> from the State Department to the Library of Congress. Putnam expanded foreign acquisitions as well, including the 1904 purchase of a four-thousand volume library of <a href="/wiki/Indica_(Megasthenes)" title="Indica (Megasthenes)">Indica</a>, the 1906 purchase of G. V. Yudin's eighty-thousand volume Russian library, the 1908 Schatz collection of early opera <a href="/wiki/Librettos" class="mw-redirect" title="Librettos">librettos</a>, and the early 1930s purchase of the Russian Imperial Collection, consisting of 2,600 volumes from the library of the <a href="/wiki/Romanov_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Romanov family">Romanov family</a> on a variety of topics. Collections of <a href="/wiki/Hebraica" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebraica">Hebraica</a> and Chinese and Japanese works were also acquired.  Congress even took the initiative to acquire materials for the library in one occasion, when in 1929 Congressman <a href="/wiki/Ross_Collins" class="mw-redirect" title="Ross Collins">Ross Collins</a> of Mississippi successfully proposed the $1.5 million purchase of Otto Vollbehr's collection of <a href="/wiki/Incunabula" class="mw-redirect" title="Incunabula">incunabula</a>, including one of three remaining perfect vellum copies of the <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg_Bible" title="Gutenberg Bible">Gutenberg Bible</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-loc_history_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc_history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gutenberg_Bible.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Gutenberg bible open to page" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Gutenberg_Bible.jpg/220px-Gutenberg_Bible.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Gutenberg_Bible.jpg/330px-Gutenberg_Bible.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Gutenberg_Bible.jpg/440px-Gutenberg_Bible.jpg 2x" data-file-width="925" data-file-height="625" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gutenberg_Bible.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A copy of the <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg_Bible" title="Gutenberg Bible">Gutenberg Bible</a> on display at the Library of Congress</div></div></div>
<p>In 1914, Putnam established the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Reference_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="Legislative Reference Service">Legislative Reference Service</a> as a separative administrative unit of the library. Based in the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Progressive era">Progressive era</a>'s philosophy of science as a problem-solver, and modeled after successful research branches of state legislatures, the LRS would provide informed answers to Congressional research inquiries on almost any topic.  In 1965, Congress passed an act allowing the Library of Congress to establish a trust fund board to accept donations and endowments, giving the library a role as a patron of the arts. The library received the donations and endowments of prominent individuals such as <a href="/wiki/John_D._Rockefeller" title="John D. Rockefeller">John D. Rockefeller</a>, James B. Wilbur and <a href="/wiki/Archer_M._Huntington" class="mw-redirect" title="Archer M. Huntington">Archer M. Huntington</a>. Gertrude Clarke Whittall donated five <a href="/wiki/List_of_Stradivarius_instruments#Violins" title="List of Stradivarius instruments">Stradivarius violins</a> to the library and <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Sprague_Coolidge" title="Elizabeth Sprague Coolidge">Elizabeth Sprague Coolidge</a>'s donations paid for a <a href="/wiki/Concert_hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Concert hall">concert hall</a> within the Library of Congress building and the establishment of an <a href="/wiki/Honorarium" title="Honorarium">honorarium</a> for the Music Division. A number of chairs and consultantships were established from the donations, the most well-known of which is the <a href="/wiki/Poet_Laureate_Consultant_in_Poetry_to_the_Library_of_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="Poet Laureate Consultant in Poetry to the Library of Congress">Poet Laureate Consultant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-loc_history_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc_history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The library's expansion eventually filled the library's Main Building, despite shelving expansions in 1910 and 1927, forcing the library to expand into a new structure. Congress acquired nearby land in 1928 and approved construction of the Annex Building (later the John Adams Building) in 1930. Although delayed during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Depression</a> years, it was completed in 1938 and opened to the public in 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-loc_history_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc_history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1939.E2.80.93present:_Modern_history"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1939–present:_Modern_history">1939–present: Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1939–present: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Erotica_at_Library_of_Congress,_Washington_D.C._02223u_original.jpg" class="image"><img alt="mural painting titled Erotica, by George Randolph Bars" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Erotica_at_Library_of_Congress%2C_Washington_D.C._02223u_original.jpg/170px-Erotica_at_Library_of_Congress%2C_Washington_D.C._02223u_original.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Erotica_at_Library_of_Congress%2C_Washington_D.C._02223u_original.jpg/255px-Erotica_at_Library_of_Congress%2C_Washington_D.C._02223u_original.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Erotica_at_Library_of_Congress%2C_Washington_D.C._02223u_original.jpg/340px-Erotica_at_Library_of_Congress%2C_Washington_D.C._02223u_original.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4768" data-file-height="6578" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Erotica_at_Library_of_Congress,_Washington_D.C._02223u_original.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Erotica</i>, mural painting by <a href="/wiki/George_Randolph_Barse" title="George Randolph Barse">George Randolph Barse</a> (1861–1938) in the Library of Congress</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Minerva_of_Peace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="mosaic wall decoration Minerva of Peace mosaic by Elihu Vedder" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Minerva_of_Peace.jpg/170px-Minerva_of_Peace.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Minerva_of_Peace.jpg/255px-Minerva_of_Peace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Minerva_of_Peace.jpg/340px-Minerva_of_Peace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1543" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Minerva_of_Peace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Elihu_Vedder" title="Elihu Vedder">Elihu Vedder</a>'s <i>Minerva of Peace mosaic</i></div></div></div>
<p>When Putnam retired in 1939, President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> appointed <a href="/wiki/Archibald_MacLeish" title="Archibald MacLeish">Archibald MacLeish</a> as his successor. Occupying the post from 1939 to 1944 during the height of World War II, MacLeish became the most visible librarian of Congress in the library's history. MacLeish encouraged librarians to oppose <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarianism</a> on behalf of democracy; dedicated the South Reading Room of the Adams Building to Thomas Jefferson, commissioning artist <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Winter" title="Ezra Winter">Ezra Winter</a> to paint four themed murals for the room; and established a "democracy alcove" in the Main Reading Room of the Jefferson Building for important documents such as the Declaration, Constitution and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Federalist_Papers" title="The Federalist Papers">The Federalist Papers</a></i>. The Library of Congress even assisted during the war effort, ranging from the storage of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fort_Knox" title="Fort Knox">Fort Knox</a> for safekeeping to researching weather data on the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> for <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">Air Force</a> pilots.  MacLeish resigned in 1944 to become Assistant Secretary of State, and President <a href="/wiki/Harry_Truman" class="mw-redirect" title="Harry Truman">Harry Truman</a> appointed <a href="/wiki/Luther_H._Evans" title="Luther H. Evans">Luther H. Evans</a> as librarian of Congress. Evans, who served until 1953, expanded the library's acquisitions, cataloging and bibliographic services as much as the fiscal-minded Congress would allow, but his primary achievement was the creation of Library of Congress Missions around the world. Missions played a variety of roles in the postwar world: the mission in San Francisco assisted participants in the <a href="/wiki/Meeting_that_established_the_United_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Meeting that established the United Nations">meeting that established the United Nations</a>, the mission in Europe acquired European publications for the Library of Congress and other American libraries, and the mission in Japan aided in the creation of the <a href="/wiki/National_Diet_Library" title="National Diet Library">National Diet Library</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-loc_history_7-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc_history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> 
Evans' successor <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Quincy_Mumford" title="Lawrence Quincy Mumford">Lawrence Quincy Mumford</a> took over in 1953. Mumford's tenure, lasting until 1974, saw the initiation of the construction of the James Madison Memorial Building, the third Library of Congress building. Mumford directed the library during a period of increased educational spending, the windfall of which allowed the library to devote energies towards establishing new acquisition centers abroad, including in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a> and New Delhi. In 1967, the library began experimenting with <a href="/wiki/Book_preservation" class="mw-redirect" title="Book preservation">book preservation</a> techniques through a Preservation Office, which grew to become the largest library research and conservation effort in the United States.  Mumford's administration also saw the last major public debate about the Library of Congress' role as both a legislative library and a national library. A 1962 memorandum by Douglas Bryant of the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Library" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard University Library">Harvard University Library</a>, compiled at the request of Joint Library Committee chairman <a href="/wiki/Claiborne_Pell" title="Claiborne Pell">Claiborne Pell</a>, proposed a number of institutional reforms, including expansion of national activities and services and various organizational changes, all of which would shift the library more towards its national role over its legislative role. Bryant even suggested possibly changing the name of the Library of Congress, which was rebuked by Mumford as "unspeakable violence to tradition".  Debate continued within the library community until the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Reorganization_Act_of_1970" title="Legislative Reorganization Act of 1970">Legislative Reorganization Act of 1970</a> shifted the library back towards its legislative roles, placing greater focus on research for Congress and congressional committees and renaming the Legislative Reference Service to the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Research_Service" title="Congressional Research Service">Congressional Research Service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-loc_history_7-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc_history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After Mumford retired in 1974, Gerald Ford appointed <a href="/wiki/Daniel_J._Boorstin" title="Daniel J. Boorstin">Daniel J. Boorstin</a> as librarian. Boorstin's first challenge was the move to the new Madison Building, which took place between 1980 and 1982. The move released pressures on staff and shelf space, allowing Boorstin to focus on other areas of library administration such as acquisitions and collections. Taking advantage of steady budgetary growth, from $116 million in 1975 to over $250 million by 1987, Boorstin actively participated in enhancing ties with scholars, authors, publishers, cultural leaders, and the business community. His active and prolific role changed the post of librarian of Congress so that by the time he retired in 1987, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> called it "perhaps the leading intellectual public position in the nation".
</p><p>President Ronald Reagan nominated <a href="/wiki/James_H._Billington" title="James H. Billington">James H. Billington</a> as the 13th librarian of Congress in 1987, and the U.S. Senate unanimously confirmed the appointment.<sup id="cite_ref-loc.gov_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc.gov-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Under Billington's leadership, the library doubled the size of its analog collections from 85.5 million items in 1987 to more than 160 million items in 2014. At the same time, it established new programs and employed new technologies to, "get the champagne out of the bottle." These included:
</p>
<ul><li><b>American Memory</b> created in 1990, which became <b>The National Digital Library</b> in 1994, providing free access online to digitized American history and culture resources with curatorial explanations for <a href="/wiki/K-12_education" class="mw-redirect" title="K-12 education">K-12 education</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>thomas.gov website</b> launched in 1994 to provide free public access to U.S. federal legislative information with ongoing updates; and <b>congress.gov website</b> to provide a state-of-the-art framework for both Congress and the public in 2012;<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>The National Book Festival</b>, founded in 2000 with <a href="/wiki/Laura_Bush" title="Laura Bush">Laura Bush</a>, has brought over 1000 authors and a million guests to the National Mall and the Washington Convention Center to celebrate reading. With a major gift from <a href="/wiki/David_Rubenstein" title="David Rubenstein">David Rubenstein</a> in 2013, the library also established the Library of Congress Literacy Awards to recognize and support achievements in improving literacy in the U.S. and abroad;<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>The Kluge Center</b>, started with a grant of $60 million from John W. Kluge in 2000 to bring scholars and researchers from around the world to use library resources and to interact with policymakers and the public. It hosts public lectures and scholarly events, provides endowed Kluge fellowships, and awards <b>The Kluge Prize for the Study of Humanity</b> (now worth $1.5 million), the first Nobel-level international prize for lifetime achievement in the humanities and social sciences (subjects not included in the Nobel awards);<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Open World Leadership Center</b>, established in 2000, administered 23,000 professional exchanges for emerging post-Soviet leaders in Russia, Ukraine and the other successor states of the former USSR by 2015. Open World began as a Library of Congress project, and later became an independent agency in the legislative branch.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>The Veterans History Project,</b> congressionally mandated in 2000 to collect, preserve, and make accessible the personal accounts of American war veterans from WWI to the present day;<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>The National Audio-Visual Conservation Center</b>, which opened in 2007 at a 45-acre site in Culpeper, Virginia with the largest private gift ever made to the library (more than $150 million by the Packard Humanities Institute) and $82.1 million additional support from Congress. In 1988, The library also established the National Film Preservation Board, a congressionally mandated National Film Preservation Board to select American films annually for preservation and inclusion in the new National Registry. The librarian named 650 films to the registry by 2015;<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>The Gershwin Prize for Popular Song,</b><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> launched in 2007 to honor the work of an artist whose career reflects lifetime achievement in song composition. Winners have included <a href="/wiki/Paul_Simon" title="Paul Simon">Paul Simon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stevie_Wonder" title="Stevie Wonder">Stevie Wonder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burt_Bacharach" title="Burt Bacharach">Burt Bacharach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hal_David" title="Hal David">Hal David</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carole_King" title="Carole King">Carole King</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billy_Joel" title="Billy Joel">Billy Joel</a>, and just-named <a href="/wiki/Willie_Nelson" title="Willie Nelson">Willie Nelson</a> for November 2015. The library also launched the <b><a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress_Living_Legend" title="Library of Congress Living Legend">Living Legend Awards</a></b> in 2000 to honor artists, activists, filmmakers, and others who have contributed to America's diverse cultural, scientific, and social heritage;</li>
<li><b>The Fiction Prize (now the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress_Prize_for_American_Fiction" title="Library of Congress Prize for American Fiction">Library of Congress Prize for American Fiction</a>)</b> started in 2008 to recognize distinguished lifetime achievement in the writing of fiction.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>The World Digital Library</b>, established in association with UNESCO and 181 partners in 81 countries in 2009, to make online copies of professionally curated primary materials of the world's varied cultures freely available in multiple languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Background_-_World_Digital_Library_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Background_-_World_Digital_Library-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Background_-_World_Digital_Library_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Background_-_World_Digital_Library-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>National Jukebox</b> launched in 2011 to provide streaming free online access to more than 10,000 out-of-print music and spoken word recordings.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>BARD</b> in 2013, digital talking books mobile app for Braille and Audio Reading Downloads in partnership with the library's National Library Service for the blind and physically handicapped, that enables free downloads of audio and <a href="/wiki/Braille" title="Braille">Braille</a> books to mobile devices via the <a href="/wiki/App_Store_(iOS)" title="App Store (iOS)">Apple App Store</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>During Billington's tenure as the 13th librarian of Congress, the library acquired Lafayette's previously inaccessible papers in 1996 from a castle at La Grange, France; and the only copy of the 1507 <a href="/wiki/Waldseem%C3%BCller_map" title="Waldseemüller map">Waldseemüller</a> world map ("America's birth certificate") in 2003 for permanent display in the library's Thomas Jefferson Building. Using privately raised funds, the Library of Congress reconstructed Thomas Jefferson's original library, which was placed on permanent display in the Jefferson building in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Billington also enlarged and technologically enhanced public spaces of the Jefferson Building into a national exhibition venue, and hosted over 100 exhibitions.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> These included exhibits on the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_Library" title="Vatican Library">Vatican Library</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale_de_France" title="Bibliothèque nationale de France">Bibliothèque Nationale de France</a>, several on the Civil War and Lincoln, on African-American culture, on Religion and the founding of the American Republic, the Early Americas (the Kislak Collection became a permanent display), on the global celebration commemorating the 800th anniversary of <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>, and on early American printing featuring the Rubenstein <a href="/wiki/Bay_Psalm_Book" title="Bay Psalm Book">Bay Psalm Book</a>. Onsite access to the Library of Congress was also increased when Billington advocated successfully for an underground connection between the U.S. Capitol Visitors Center and the library in 2008 to increase congressional usage and public tours of the library's Thomas Jefferson Building.<sup id="cite_ref-loc.gov_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc.gov-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under Billington, the library launched a <a href="/wiki/Mass_deacidification" title="Mass deacidification">mass deacidification</a> program in 2001, which has extended the lifespan of almost 4 million volumes and 12 million manuscript sheets; and new collection storage modules at Fort Meade, the first opening in 2002, to preserve and make accessible more than 4 million items from the library's analog collections. Billington established the Library Collections Security Oversight Committee in 1992 to improve protection of collections, and also the Library of Congress Congressional Caucus in 2008 to draw attention to the library's curators and collections. He created the library's first Young Readers Center in the Jefferson Building in 2009, and the first large-scale summer intern (Junior Fellows) program for university
students in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Under Billington, the library also sponsored the Gateway to Knowledge in 2010–2011, a mobile exhibition to 90 sites covering all states east of the Mississippi in a specially designed 18-wheel truck, increasing public access to library collections off-site, particularly for rural populations.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Billington raised more than half a billion dollars of private support to supplement Congressional appropriations for library collections, programs, and digital outreach. These private funds helped the library to continue its growth and outreach in the face of a 30% decrease in staffing caused mainly by legislative appropriations cutbacks. He created the library's first development office for private fundraising in 1987, and, in 1990, established the James Madison Council, the library's first national private sector donor-support group. In 1987, Billington also asked the GAO to conduct the first library-wide audit, and he created the first Office of the Inspector General at the library to provide regular independent review of library operations. This precedent led to regular annual financial audits, leading to unmodified ("clean") opinions from 1995 onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-loc.gov_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc.gov-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2010, it announced plans to archive all public communication on <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>, including all communication since Twitter's launch in March 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-Grier_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grier-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the Twitter archive remains unfinished.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Before retiring in 2015, after 28 years of service, Billington had come "under pressure" as librarian of Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> This followed a <a href="/wiki/Government_Accountability_Office" title="Government Accountability Office">Government Accountability Office</a> report which revealed a "work environment lacking central oversight" and faulted Billington for "ignoring repeated calls to hire a chief information officer, as required by law."<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When Billington announced his plans to retire in 2015, commentator George Weigel described the Library of Congress as "one of the last refuges in Washington of serious bipartisanship and calm, considered conversation," and "one of the world's greatest cultural centers."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Carla Hayden was sworn in as the 14th librarian of Congress on September 14, 2016, becoming both the first woman and the first African-American to hold the position.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, the library announced the Librarian-in-Residence program which aims to support the future generation of librarians by giving them opportunity to gain work experience in five different areas of librarianship including: Acquisitions/Collection Development, Cataloging/Metadata, and Collection Preservation.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Holdings">Holdings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Holdings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Library_of_Congress_and_lawn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="photograph of west colonnade by Carol M. Highsmith" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Library_of_Congress_and_lawn.jpg/220px-Library_of_Congress_and_lawn.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Library_of_Congress_and_lawn.jpg/330px-Library_of_Congress_and_lawn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Library_of_Congress_and_lawn.jpg/440px-Library_of_Congress_and_lawn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3600" data-file-height="2166" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Library_of_Congress_and_lawn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Library of Congress, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Building" title="Thomas Jefferson Building">Thomas Jefferson Building</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson_Great_Hall_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="photograph of the Great Hall in the Thomas Jefferson building by Carol M. Highsmith" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Thomas_Jefferson_Great_Hall_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg/220px-Thomas_Jefferson_Great_Hall_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Thomas_Jefferson_Great_Hall_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg/330px-Thomas_Jefferson_Great_Hall_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Thomas_Jefferson_Great_Hall_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg/440px-Thomas_Jefferson_Great_Hall_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1214" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson_Great_Hall_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Great Hall interior</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Library_Congress_October_2016-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Library_Congress_October_2016-1.jpg/220px-Library_Congress_October_2016-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Library_Congress_October_2016-1.jpg/330px-Library_Congress_October_2016-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Library_Congress_October_2016-1.jpg/440px-Library_Congress_October_2016-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6472" data-file-height="4351" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Library_Congress_October_2016-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ceiling of the Great Hall</div></div></div>
<p>The collections of the Library of Congress include more than 32 million catalogued books and other print materials in 470 languages; more than 61 million <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscripts</a>; the largest rare book collection<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> in North America, including the rough draft of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg_Bible" title="Gutenberg Bible">Gutenberg Bible</a> (originating from the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Blaise_Abbey,_Black_Forest" title="Saint Blaise Abbey, Black Forest">Saint Blaise Abbey, Black Forest</a>) (one of only three perfect <a href="/wiki/Vellum" title="Vellum">vellum</a> copies known to exist);<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> over 1 million <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">U.S. government</a> publications; 1 million issues of world newspapers spanning the past three centuries; 33,000 bound newspaper volumes; 500,000 <a href="/wiki/Microfilm" class="mw-redirect" title="Microfilm">microfilm</a> reels; over 6,000 titles in all, totaling more than 120,000 issues comic book<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> titles; films; 5.3 million <a href="/wiki/Map" title="Map">maps</a>; 6 million works of <a href="/wiki/Sheet_music" title="Sheet music">sheet music</a>; 3 million <a href="/wiki/Sound_recording" class="mw-redirect" title="Sound recording">sound recordings</a>; more than 14.7 million prints and photographic images including fine and popular art pieces and architectural drawings;<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Betts_Stradivarius" title="Betts Stradivarius">Betts Stradivarius</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Stradivarius_instruments#Violas" title="List of Stradivarius instruments">Cassavetti Stradivarius</a>.
</p><p>The library developed a system of book classification called <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress_Classification" title="Library of Congress Classification">Library of Congress Classification</a> (LCC), which is used by most US research and <a href="/wiki/University_library" class="mw-redirect" title="University library">university libraries</a>.
</p><p>The library serves as a legal repository for <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">copyright</a> protection and <a href="/wiki/Copyright_registration" title="Copyright registration">copyright registration</a>, and as the base for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Copyright_Office" title="United States Copyright Office">United States Copyright Office</a>. Regardless of whether they register their copyright, all publishers are required to submit two complete copies of their published works to the library—this requirement is known as <i>mandatory deposit</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Nearly 15,000 new items published in the U.S. arrive every business day at the library. Contrary to popular belief, however, the library does not retain all of these works in its permanent collection, although it does add an average of 12,000 items per day.<sup id="cite_ref-Fascinating_Facts_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fascinating_Facts-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Rejected items are used in trades with other libraries around the world, distributed to federal agencies, or donated to schools, communities, and other organizations within the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Fascinating_Facts_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fascinating_Facts-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> As is true of many <a href="/wiki/Legal_deposit" title="Legal deposit">similar libraries</a>, the Library of Congress retains copies of every publication in the English language that is deemed significant.
</p><p>The Library of Congress states that its collection fills about 838 miles (1,349&#160;km) of bookshelves,<sup id="cite_ref-FFStats_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FFStats-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a> reports about 388 miles (624&#160;km) of shelves.<sup id="cite_ref-aboutbrit_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aboutbrit-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The Library of Congress holds more than 167 million items with more than 39 million books and other print materials,<sup id="cite_ref-FFStats_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FFStats-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> against approximately 150 million items with 25 million books for the British Library.<sup id="cite_ref-aboutbrit_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aboutbrit-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> A 2000 study by information scientists <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lyman" title="Peter Lyman">Peter Lyman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hal_Varian" title="Hal Varian">Hal Varian</a> suggested that the amount of uncompressed <a href="/wiki/Text_file" title="Text file">textual data</a> represented by the 26 million books then in the collection was 10 <a href="/wiki/Terabyte" title="Terabyte">terabytes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The library also administers the <a href="/wiki/National_Library_Service_for_the_Blind_and_Physically_Handicapped" class="mw-redirect" title="National Library Service for the Blind and Physically Handicapped">National Library Service for the Blind and Physically Handicapped</a>, an audio book and <a href="/wiki/Braille" title="Braille">braille</a> library program provided to more than 766,000 Americans.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digitization">Digitization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Digitization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The library's first digitization project was called "<a href="/wiki/American_Memory" title="American Memory">American Memory</a>." Launched in 1990, it initially planned to choose 160 million objects from its collection to make digitally available on <a href="/wiki/LaserDisc" title="LaserDisc">laserdiscs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Compact_disc" title="Compact disc">CDs</a> that would be distributed to schools and libraries. After realizing that this plan would be too expensive and inefficient, and with the rise of the Internet, the library decided to instead make digitized material available over the Internet. This project was made official in the National Digital Library Program (NDLP), created in October 1994. By 1999, the NDLP had succeeded in digitizing over 5 million objects and had a budget of $12 million. The library has kept the "American Memory" name for its public domain website, which today contains 15 million digital objects, comprising over 7 <a href="/wiki/Petabyte" title="Petabyte">petabytes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>American Memory is a source for <a href="/wiki/Public_domain" title="Public domain">public domain</a> image resources, as well as audio, video, and archived Web content. Nearly all of the lists of holdings, the <i>catalogs</i> of the library, can be consulted directly on its web site. Librarians all over the world consult these catalogs, through the Web or through other media better suited to their needs, when they need to catalog for their collection a book published in the United States. They use the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress_Control_Number" title="Library of Congress Control Number">Library of Congress Control Number</a> to make sure of the exact identity of the book. Digital images are also available at <a href="/wiki/Snapshots_of_the_Past" title="Snapshots of the Past">Snapshots of the Past</a>, which provides archival prints.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The library has a budget of between $6–8 million each year for digitization, meaning that not all works can be digitized. It makes determinations about what objects to prioritize based on what is especially important to Congress or potentially interesting for the public. The 15 million digitized items represent less than 10% of the library's total 160-million item collection.
</p><p>The library has chosen not to participate in other digital library projects such as <a href="/wiki/Google_Books" title="Google Books">Google Books</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Digital_Public_Library_of_America" title="Digital Public Library of America">Digital Public Library of America</a>, although it has supported the <a href="/wiki/Internet_Archive" title="Internet Archive">Internet Archive</a> project.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="THOMAS_and_Congress.gov_projects">THOMAS and Congress.gov projects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: THOMAS and Congress.gov projects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1995, the Library of Congress established online archive of the proceedings of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">U.S. Congress</a>, <a href="/wiki/THOMAS" title="THOMAS">THOMAS</a>. The THOMAS website included the full text of proposed legislation, as well as bill summaries and statuses, <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Record" title="Congressional Record">Congressional Record</a> text, and the Congressional Record Index. The THOMAS system received major updates in 2005 and 2010. A migration to a more modernized Web system, <a href="/wiki/Congress.gov" title="Congress.gov">Congress.gov</a>, began in 2012, and the THOMAS system was retired in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Congress.gov is a joint project of the Library of Congress, the House, the Senate and the <a href="/wiki/Government_Publishing_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="Government Publishing Office">Government Publishing Office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Library_of_Congress_buildings">Library of Congress buildings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Library of Congress buildings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson_Building_Aerial_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Aerial photograph of the Thomas Jefferson Building by Carol M. Highsmith" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Thomas_Jefferson_Building_Aerial_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg/220px-Thomas_Jefferson_Building_Aerial_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Thomas_Jefferson_Building_Aerial_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg/330px-Thomas_Jefferson_Building_Aerial_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Thomas_Jefferson_Building_Aerial_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg/440px-Thomas_Jefferson_Building_Aerial_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="686" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson_Building_Aerial_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Building" title="Thomas Jefferson Building">Thomas Jefferson Building</a> and part of the John Adams Building (upper-right)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Adams_Building_(31274182293).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/John_Adams_Building_%2831274182293%29.jpg/220px-John_Adams_Building_%2831274182293%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/John_Adams_Building_%2831274182293%29.jpg/330px-John_Adams_Building_%2831274182293%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/John_Adams_Building_%2831274182293%29.jpg/440px-John_Adams_Building_%2831274182293%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2500" data-file-height="1638" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Adams_Building_(31274182293).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The John Adams Building of the Library of Congress</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Madison_Building_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="northeast photograph of Madison Building by Carol M. Highsmith" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Madison_Building_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg/220px-Madison_Building_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Madison_Building_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg/330px-Madison_Building_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Madison_Building_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg/440px-Madison_Building_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="545" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Madison_Building_by_Carol_M._Highsmith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_Madison_Memorial_Building" title="James Madison Memorial Building">Madison Building</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Packard-campus-library-of-c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="photograph of the National Audio-Visual Conservation Center Packard Campus in Culpeper, Virginia" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Packard-campus-library-of-c.jpg/220px-Packard-campus-library-of-c.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="106" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Packard-campus-library-of-c.jpg/330px-Packard-campus-library-of-c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Packard-campus-library-of-c.jpg/440px-Packard-campus-library-of-c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="347" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Packard-campus-library-of-c.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/National_Audio-Visual_Conservation_Center" title="National Audio-Visual Conservation Center">Packard Campus (Culpeper, Virginia)</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Library of Congress is physically housed in three buildings on Capitol Hill and a conservation center in rural <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>. The library's Capitol Hill buildings are all connected by underground passageways, so that a library user need pass through security only once in a single visit. The library also has off-site storage facilities for less commonly requested materials.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thomas_Jefferson_Building">Thomas Jefferson Building</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Thomas Jefferson Building">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Building" title="Thomas Jefferson Building">Thomas Jefferson Building</a></div>
<p>The Thomas Jefferson Building is located between <a href="/wiki/Independence_Avenue_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Independence Avenue (Washington, D.C.)">Independence Avenue</a> and East Capitol Street on First Street SE. It first opened in 1897 as the main building of the library and is the oldest of the three buildings. Known originally as the Library of Congress Building or Main Building, it took its present name on June 13, 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="John_Adams_Building">John Adams Building</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: John Adams Building">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/John_Adams_Building" title="John Adams Building">John Adams Building</a></div>
<p>The John Adams Building is located between Independence Avenue and East Capitol Street on 2nd Street SE, the block adjacent to the Jefferson Building. The building was originally known as The Annex to the Main Building, which had run out of space. It opened its doors to the public January 3, 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="James_Madison_Memorial_Building">James Madison Memorial Building</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Library_of_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: James Madison Memorial Building">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/James_Madison_Memorial_Building" title="James Madison Memorial Building">James Madison Memorial Building</a></div>
<p>The James Madison Memorial Building is located between First and Second Streets on Independence Avenue SE. The building was constructed from 1971 to 1976, and serves as the official memorial to President James Madison.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Madison Building is also home to the Mary Pickford Theater, the "motion picture and television reading room" of the Library of Congress.
